<p><b>Edsger Wybe Dijkstra</b> (<small>Dutch: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Dutch" title="Help:IPA for Dutch">[ˈɛtsxər ˈʋibə ˈdɛikstra]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata"> (<a href="/wiki/File:Dijkstra.ogg" title="File:Dijkstra.ogg"><img alt="" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/20px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/26px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 2x" width="13"/></a> <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/85/Dijkstra.ogg" title="Dijkstra.ogg">listen</a>)</small>; 11 May 1930 – 6 August 2002) was a Dutch <a href="/wiki/Computer_scientist" title="Computer scientist">computer scientist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> A theoretical physicist by training, he worked as a <a href="/wiki/Programmer" title="Programmer">programmer</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a> (Amsterdam) from 1952 to 1962. He was a professor of mathematics at the <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven_University_of_Technology" title="Eindhoven University of Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</a> (1962–1984) and a research fellow at the <a href="/wiki/Burroughs_Corporation" title="Burroughs Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</a> (1973–1984). He held the Schlumberger Centennial Chair in Computer Sciences at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> from 1984 until 1999, and retired as Professor Emeritus in 1999.</p>
<p>One of the most influential members of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computing_science" title="Computing science">computing science</a>'s <a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">founding generation</a>, Dijkstra helped shape the new discipline from both an engineering and a <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">theoretical perspective</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup> His fundamental contributions cover diverse areas of computing science, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compiler_construction" title="Compiler construction">compiler construction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operating_systems" title="Operating systems">operating systems</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributed_systems" title="Distributed systems">distributed systems</a>, sequential and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concurrent_programming" title="Concurrent programming">concurrent programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Programming_paradigm" title="Programming paradigm">programming paradigm</a> and methodology, <a href="/wiki/Programming_language_theory" title="Programming language theory">programming language</a> research, <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">program design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_development" title="Software development">program development</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Program_verification" title="Program verification">program verification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> principles, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graph_algorithms" title="Graph algorithms">graph algorithms</a>, and philosophical foundations of computer science and computer programming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Many of his papers are the source of new research areas. Several concepts and problems that are now standard in computer science were first identified by Dijkstra and/or bear names coined by him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">Computer programming</a> in the 1950s to 1960s was not recognized as an academic discipline and unlike physics there were no theoretical concepts or coding systems. Dijkstra was one of the moving forces behind the acceptance of computer programming as a scientific discipline. A training background in mathematics and physics led to his applying similar disciplines of mathematical logic and methodology to computer programming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DijkstraNYT_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraNYT-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In 1968, computer programming was in a <a href="/wiki/Software_crisis" title="Software crisis">state of crisis</a>. Dijkstra was one of a small group of academics and industrial programmers who advocated a new <a href="/wiki/Programming_style" title="Programming style">programming style</a> to improve the quality of programs. Dijkstra coined the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming</a>" and during the 1970s this became the new programming orthodoxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Dijkstra's ideas about structured programming helped lay the foundations for the birth and development of the professional discipline of <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a>, enabling programmers to organize and manage increasingly complex software projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dijkstra was an early theoretical pioneer in many research areas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computing_science" title="Computing science">computing science</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Algorithm_design" title="Algorithm design">algorithm design</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" title="Programming methodology">programming methodology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Software_architecture" title="Software architecture">software architecture</a>. The academic study of <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">concurrent computing</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concurrent_programming" title="Concurrent programming">concurrent programming</a> started in the 1960s, with Dijkstra (1965) credited with being the first paper in this field, identifying and solving the <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> He was also one of the early pioneers of the research on principles of <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a>. His foundational work on <a href="/wiki/Concurrency_(computer_science)" title="Concurrency (computer science)">concurrency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> (mutex), <a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a> (deadly embrace), finding <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">shortest paths in graphs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fault-tolerance" title="Fault-tolerance">fault-tolerance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a>, among many other contributions comprises many of the pillars upon which the field of <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> is built. Shortly before his death in 2002, he received the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">ACM</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PODC_Influential-Paper_Award" title="PODC Influential-Paper Award">PODC Influential-Paper Award</a> in distributed computing for his work on self-stabilization of program computation. This annual award was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra_Prize" title="Dijkstra Prize">Dijkstra Prize</a> (Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing) the following year, in his honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Edsger W. Dijkstra was born in <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>. His father was a chemist who was president of the Dutch Chemical Society; he taught chemistry at a secondary school and was later its superintendent. His mother was a mathematician, but never had a formal job.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dijkstra had considered a career in law during his last years in high school. He had hoped to represent the Netherlands in the United Nations. He graduated in 1948 with the highest possible marks in mathematics, physics, chemistry, and biology. So his teachers and parents counseled him to seek a career in the sciences. He decided to join the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Leiden" title="University of Leiden">University of Leiden</a>, to study mathematics and physics during the first years, and <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a> later on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 1950s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_computers" title="Electronic computers">electronic computers</a> were a novelty. Dijkstra stumbled on his career quite by accident. His father had seen an advertisement for a three-week course in programming for the electronic computer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EDSAC" title="EDSAC">EDSAC</a>, to be given at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a>, and he advised his son to attend the course. Dijkstra felt that computers might become an important tool for theoretical physicists, and he decided to attend this course in September 1951. When he asked his supervisor, Professor dr. A. Haantjes, for a letter of recommendation, it turned out that Professor Haantjes knew <a href="/wiki/Adriaan_van_Wijngaarden" title="Adriaan van Wijngaarden">Adriaan van Wijngaarden</a>, the director of the Computation Department at the <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Mathematical Center in Amsterdam</a>, and he knew that van Wijngaarden had taken the same course in 1950. Professor Haantjes phoned van Wijngaarden who invited Dijkstra to visit the Mathematical Center and wrote the letter of recommendation. Van Wijngaarden also offered Dijkstra a job, which he accepted, becoming officially the Netherlands' first "programmer" in March 1952.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1956 he graduated from the <a href="/wiki/Leiden_University" title="Leiden University">Leiden University</a> in mathematics and theoretical physics. In 1959 he received his PhD from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Amsterdam" title="University of Amsterdam">University of Amsterdam</a> for his thesis entitled 'Communication with an Automatic Computer', devoted to a description of the assembly language designed for the first commercial computer developed in the Netherlands, the X1. It also dealt with the concept of an interrupt, a novelty at that time. His PhD thesis supervisor was van Wijngaarden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>For some time Dijkstra remained committed to physics, working on it in <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">Leiden</a> three days out of each week. With increasing exposure to computing, however, his focus began to shift. As he recalled:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ewd340_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>After having programmed for some three years, I had a discussion with <a href="/wiki/Adriaan_van_Wijngaarden" title="Adriaan van Wijngaarden">A. van Wijngaarden</a>, who was then my boss at the <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Mathematical Center in Amsterdam</a>, a discussion for which I shall remain grateful to him as long as I live. The point was that I was supposed to study <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Leiden" title="University of Leiden">University of Leiden</a> simultaneously, and as I found the two activities harder and harder to combine, I had to make up my mind, either to stop programming and become a real, respectable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theoretical_physicist" title="Theoretical physicist">theoretical physicist</a>, or to carry my study of physics to a formal completion only, with a minimum of effort, and to become....., yes what? A <a href="/wiki/Programmer" title="Programmer">programmer</a>? But was that a respectable profession? For after all, what was <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">programming</a>? Where was the sound body of knowledge that could support it as an intellectually respectable discipline? I remember quite vividly how I envied my hardware colleagues, who, when asked about their professional competence, could at least point out that they knew everything about vacuum tubes, amplifiers and the rest, whereas I felt that, when faced with that question, I would stand empty-handed. Full of misgivings I knocked on van Wijngaarden's office door, asking him whether I could "speak to him for a moment"; when I left his office a number of hours later, I was another person. For after having listened to my problems patiently, he agreed that up till that moment there was not much of a programming discipline, but then he went on to explain quietly that automatic computers were here to stay, that we were just at the beginning and could not I be one of the persons called to make programming a respectable discipline in the years to come? This was a turning point in my life and I completed my study of physics formally as quickly as I could.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Edsger Dijkstra, The Humble Programmer (EWD340)</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>When Dijkstra married Maria (Ria) C. Debets in 1957, he was required as a part of the marriage rites to state his profession. He stated that he was a programmer, which was unacceptable to the authorities, there being no such profession at that time in The Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematisch_Centrum.2C_Amsterdam">Mathematisch Centrum, Amsterdam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mathematisch Centrum, Amsterdam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="139" data-file-width="309" height="81" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4d/Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png/180px-Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4d/Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png/270px-Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4d/Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">CWI</a>'s logo. Dijkstra worked as a <a href="/wiki/Programmer" title="Programmer">programmer</a> at CWI (<a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica</a> / <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>) from 1952 to 1962.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1952 until 1962 Dijkstra worked at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a> in Amsterdam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup> The early computers were built out of vacuum tubes. They occupied large amounts of space, consumed power voraciously, and failed regularly. As Dijkstra recalls, "In retrospect one can only wonder that those first machines worked at all, at least sometimes. The overwhelming problem was to get and keep the machine in working order."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>He worked closely with <a href="/wiki/Bram_Jan_Loopstra" title="Bram Jan Loopstra">Bram Jan Loopstra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carel_S._Scholten" title="Carel S. Scholten">Carel S. Scholten</a>, who had been hired to build a computer. Their mode of interaction remains a model of disciplined engineering: They would first decide upon the interface between the hardware and the software, by writing a programming manual. Then the hardware designers would have to be faithful to their part of the contract, while Dijkstra, the programmer, would write software for the nonexistent machine. Two of the lessons he learned from this experience were the importance of clear documentation, and that program debugging can be largely avoided through careful design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup> Dijkstra formulated and solved the <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">shortest path problem</a> for a demonstration at the official inauguration of the ARMAC computer in 1956, but —because of the absence of journals dedicated to automatic computing— did not publish the result until 1959.</p>
<p>At the Mathematical Center, Dijkstra and his colleague <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=J._A._Zonneveld&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="J. A. Zonneveld (page does not exist)">J. A. Zonneveld</a> developed a <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> for the programming language <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a>; it had a profound influence on his later thinking on programming as a scientific activity. He and Zonneveld had completed the implementation of the first <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a> <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> by August 1960, more than a year before a compiler was produced by another group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eindhoven_University_of_Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Eindhoven University of Technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TU_Eindhoven.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/TU_Eindhoven.jpg/180px-TU_Eindhoven.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/TU_Eindhoven.jpg/270px-TU_Eindhoven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/TU_Eindhoven.jpg/360px-TU_Eindhoven.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TU_Eindhoven.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven_University_of_Technology" title="Eindhoven University of Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a> in the south of the Netherlands, where Dijkstra was a professor of mathematics from 1962 to 1984.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuenen_Sint_Clemenskerk_A.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3174" data-file-width="2688" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Nuenen_Sint_Clemenskerk_A.jpg/180px-Nuenen_Sint_Clemenskerk_A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Nuenen_Sint_Clemenskerk_A.jpg/270px-Nuenen_Sint_Clemenskerk_A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Nuenen_Sint_Clemenskerk_A.jpg/360px-Nuenen_Sint_Clemenskerk_A.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuenen_Sint_Clemenskerk_A.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a> and Edsger W. Dijkstra were among the most notable residents in <a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a>'s history.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1962 Dijkstra moved to <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a> in the south of the Netherlands, where he became a professor in the Mathematics Department at the <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven_University_of_Technology" title="Eindhoven University of Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</a>. Then in 1964 Dijkstra and his family moved to a newly built house in <a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a>, a small town/village on the outskirts of Eindhoven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The university did not have a separate computer science department and the culture of the mathematics department did not particularly suit him. Dijkstra tried to build a group of computer scientists who could collaborate on solving problems. This was an unusual model of research for the Mathematics Department.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the late 1960s he built the <a href="/wiki/THE_multiprogramming_system" title="THE multiprogramming system">THE operating system</a> (named for the university, then known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technische_Hogeschool_Eindhoven" title="Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven">Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven</a>), which has influenced the designs of subsequent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operating_systems" title="Operating systems">operating systems</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burroughs_Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Burroughs Corporation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dijkstra joined <a href="/wiki/Burroughs_Corporation" title="Burroughs Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</a>, a company known at that time for the production of computers based on an innovative hardware architecture, as its Research Fellow in August 1973. His duties consisted of visiting some of the company's research centers a few times a year and carrying on his own research, which he did in the smallest Burroughs research facility, namely, his study on the second floor of his house in Nuenen. In fact, Dijkstra was the only research fellow of <a href="/wiki/Burroughs_Corporation" title="Burroughs Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</a> and worked for it from home, occasionally travelling to its branches in the United States. As a result, he reduced his appointment at the university to one day a week. That day, Tuesday, soon became known as the day of the famous 'Tuesday Afternoon Club', a seminar during which he discussed with his colleagues scientific articles, looking at all aspects – notation, organisation, presentation, language, content, etc. Shortly after he moved in 1984 to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> (USA), a new 'branch' of the Tuesday Afternoon Club emerged in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was already famous by that time, and he received a large number of invitations to lecture throughout the world. He used these visits to interact with other computer scientists, mentor younger scientists, and sharpen his skills as an English speaker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Burroughs years saw him at his most prolific in output of research articles. He wrote nearly 500 documents in the EWD series (described below), most of them technical reports, for private circulation within a select group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin">The University of Texas at Austin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The University of Texas at Austin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg/180px-Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg/270px-Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg/360px-Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a>, where Dijkstra held the Schlumberger Centennial Chair in Computer Sciences from 1984 until 1999.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Dijkstra accepted the Schlumberger Centennial Chair in the Computer Science Department at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> in 1984. He had been visiting the Burroughs Research Center in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a> since the late 1970s and had become quite familiar with the Department of Computer Science and its faculty. As he says in an autobiographical note, "...when The University of Texas at Austin offered me the Schlumberger Centennial Chair in Computer Sciences, the offer was actually welcome. The offer was not so surprising, nor my acceptance, for I knew Austin, I knew UT and they knew me. All through my years at Burroughs I had made it a rule to visit, wherever I went, the local university and since the late 70s Burroughs had had an Austin Research Center, a visit to which was included in most of my trips to the USA."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Dijkstra remained prolific in research during his years at Austin. He had embarked on a long-term project on "Streamlining Mathematical Arguments".</p>
<p>The years in Austin saw Dijkstra at his best as a teacher and mentor for a generation of students, both undergraduate and graduate. From his days in the Eindhoven University of Technology, he had thought deeply about how computer science should be taught. He had sharpened his thinking on education over a number of years, and Austin provided him the opportunity for trying out his ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years">Last years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Last years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dijkstra worked in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a> until his retirement in November 1999. To mark the occasion and to celebrate his forty-plus years of seminal contributions to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computing_science" title="Computing science">computing science</a>, the Department of Computer Sciences organized a symposium, which took place on his 70th birthday in May 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dijkstra and his wife returned from Austin to his original house in <a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a> (Netherlands) where he found that he had only months to live. He said that he wanted to retire in Austin, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, but to die in the Netherlands. Dijkstra died on 6 August 2002 after a long struggle with cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> He and his wife Maria (Ria) Debets were survived by their three children: Marcus, Femke and the computer scientist Rutger M. Dijkstra.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_contributions_and_impacts">Scientific contributions and impacts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Scientific contributions and impacts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As an early theoretical <a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">pioneer</a> in many research areas of computing science, Dijkstra helped shape the <a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">new discipline</a> from both an engineering and an <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">academic perspective</a>. Many of his papers are the source of new research areas. Many concepts that are now standard in computer science were first identified by Dijkstra and/or bear names coined by him. Several important problems were also first formulated and solved by him. A 1994 survey of over a thousand professors of computer science was conducted to obtain a list of 38 most influential scholarly papers in the field, and Dijkstra is the author of five papers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>During his forty-plus years as a computing scientist, which included positions in both academia and industry, Dijkstra made numerous seminal contributions to many areas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computing_science" title="Computing science">computing science</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compiler_construction" title="Compiler construction">compiler construction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operating_systems" title="Operating systems">operating systems</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concurrent_programming" title="Concurrent programming">concurrent programming</a>/<a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">concurrent computing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributed_programming" title="Distributed programming">distributed programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Programming_paradigm" title="Programming paradigm">programming paradigm</a> and methodology, <a href="/wiki/Programming_language_theory" title="Programming language theory">programming language</a> research, <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">program design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_development" title="Software development">program development</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Program_verification" title="Program verification">program verification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> principles, <a href="/wiki/Algorithm_design" title="Algorithm design">algorithm design</a>, and philosophical foundations of <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">computer programming</a>. In addition, Dijkstra was intensely interested in <a href="/wiki/On_the_Cruelty_of_Really_Teaching_Computer_Science" title="On the Cruelty of Really Teaching Computer Science">teaching computer science</a>, and in the relationships between academic computing science and the <a href="/wiki/Software_industry" title="Software industry">software industry</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algorithmic_work">Algorithmic work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Algorithmic work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dijkstra_Animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="222" data-file-width="283" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Dijkstra_Animation.gif/220px-Dijkstra_Animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/Dijkstra_Animation.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dijkstra_Animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dijkstra's algorithm. It picks the unvisited vertex with the lowest-distance, calculates the distance through it to each unvisited neighbor, and updates the neighbor's distance if smaller. Mark visited (set to red) when done with neighbors.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Dijkstra's algorithmic work (especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graph_algorithms" title="Graph algorithms">graph algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithms</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithms" title="Distributed algorithms">distributed algorithms</a>) plays an important role in many areas of computing science. According to <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Lamport" title="Leslie Lamport">Leslie Lamport</a> (2002), Dijkstra "started the field of concurrent and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithms" title="Distributed algorithms">distributed algorithms</a> with his 1965 CACM paper "Solution of a Problem in Concurrent Programming Control", in which he first stated and solved the mutual exclusion problem." As Lamport explains, "that paper is probably why <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PODC" title="PODC">PODC</a> exists (...). It remains to this day the most influential paper in the field. That it did not win a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PODC_Influential-Paper_Award" title="PODC Influential-Paper Award">PODC Influential Paper Award</a> reflects an artificial separation between concurrent and distributed algorithms–a separation that has never existed in Dijkstra's work."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1959 Dijkstra published in a 3-page article 'A note on two problems in connexion with graphs' the <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a> to find the shortest path in a graph between any two given nodes, now called <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%27s_algorithm" title="Dijkstra's algorithm">Dijkstra's algorithm</a>. Its impact over the next 40 years is summarised from the article of <a href="/wiki/Mikkel_Thorup" title="Mikkel Thorup">Mikkel Thorup</a>, 'Undirected Single Source Shortest Paths with Positive Integer Weights in Linear Time' (1999): "Since 1959, all theoretical developments in SSSP [Single-Source Shortest Paths] for general directed and undirected graphs have been based on Dijkstra's algorithm." Dijkstra's algorithm is used in SPF, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shortest_Path_First" title="Shortest Path First">Shortest Path First</a>, which is used in the routing protocols <a href="/wiki/Open_Shortest_Path_First" title="Open Shortest Path First">OSPF</a> and <a href="/wiki/IS-IS" title="IS-IS">IS-IS</a>. Various modifications to Dijkstra's algorithm have been proposed by many authors using <a href="/wiki/Heuristic_(computer_science)" title="Heuristic (computer science)">heuristics</a> to reduce the run time of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shortest_path_search&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shortest path search (page does not exist)">shortest path search</a>. One of the most used heuristic algorithms is the <a href="/wiki/A*_search_algorithm" title="A* search algorithm">A* search algorithm</a>, the main goal is to reduce the run time by reducing the search space. <a href="/wiki/A*_search_algorithm" title="A* search algorithm">A* search algorithm</a> (first described by <a href="/wiki/Peter_E._Hart" title="Peter E. Hart">Peter Hart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nils_John_Nilsson" title="Nils John Nilsson">Nils Nilsson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bertram_Raphael" title="Bertram Raphael">Bertram Raphael</a> of <a href="/wiki/SRI_International" title="SRI International">Stanford Research Institute</a> in 1968)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nilsson_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-nilsson-44">[44]</a></sup> is an extension of Dijkstra's 1959 algorithm. Dijkstra thought about the <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">shortest path problem</a> when working at the <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Mathematical Center in Amsterdam</a> in 1956 as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_programmer" title="Computer programmer">programmer</a> to demonstrate capabilities of a new <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a> called <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ARMAC&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ARMAC (page does not exist)">ARMAC</a>. His objective was to choose both a problem as well as an answer (that would be produced by computer) that non-computing people could understand. He designed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%27s_algorithm" title="Dijkstra's algorithm">shortest path algorithm</a> in about 20 minutes without aid of paper and pen and later implemented it for ARMAC for a slightly simplified transportation map of 64 cities in Netherland (64 cities, so that 6 bits would suffice to represent the city in the algorithm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>A year later, he came across another problem from hardware engineers working on the institute's next computer: minimize the amount of wire needed to connect the pins on the back panel of the machine. As a solution, he re-discovered the algorithm known as <a href="/wiki/Prim%27s_algorithm" title="Prim's algorithm">Prim's</a> <a href="/wiki/Minimum_spanning_tree" title="Minimum spanning tree">minimal spanning tree algorithm</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Prim%27s_algorithm" title="Prim's algorithm">Prim's algorithm</a> was originally developed in 1930 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Czech_people" title="Czech people">Czech</a> mathematician <a href="/wiki/Vojt%C4%9Bch_Jarn%C3%ADk" title="Vojtěch Jarník">Vojtěch Jarník</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and later independently rediscovered and republished by <a href="/wiki/Robert_C._Prim" title="Robert C. Prim">Robert C. Prim</a> in 1957<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and Dijkstra in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Therefore, it is also sometimes called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DJP_algorithm" title="DJP algorithm">DJP algorithm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pr02_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pr02-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1961 Dijkstra first described the <a href="/wiki/Shunting-yard_algorithm" title="Shunting-yard algorithm">shunting-yard algorithm</a>, a method for parsing mathematical expressions specified in <a href="/wiki/Infix_notation" title="Infix notation">infix notation</a>, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a> report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> It can be used to produce output in <a href="/wiki/Reverse_Polish_notation" title="Reverse Polish notation">Reverse Polish notation</a> (RPN) or as an <a href="/wiki/Abstract_syntax_tree" title="Abstract syntax tree">abstract syntax tree</a> (AST). The <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a> was named the "shunting yard" algorithm because its operation resembles that of a <a href="/wiki/Classification_yard" title="Classification yard">railroad shunting yard</a>. The shunting-yard algorithm is commonly used to implement <a href="/wiki/Operator-precedence_parser" title="Operator-precedence parser">operator-precedence parsers</a>.</p>
<p>In 1962 or 1963 Dijkstra proposed the <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> mechanism for <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> algorithm for n processes (a generalization of <a href="/wiki/Dekker%27s_algorithm" title="Dekker's algorithm">Dekker's algorithm</a>), which was probably the first published <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithm</a> and which introduced a new area of algorithmic research. He also identified the <a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a> problem and proposed the <a href="/wiki/Banker%27s_algorithm" title="Banker's algorithm">banker's algorithm</a> that <a href="/wiki/Deadlock_prevention_algorithms" title="Deadlock prevention algorithms">prevents deadlock</a>.</p>
<p>In 1974 Dijkstra presented three <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilizing</a> algorithms for <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> on a ring. Dijkstra's work is considered to be the first to introduce and demonstrate the <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a> concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid-1970s Dijkstra (together with other authors) introduced two useful abstractions (mutator and collector) to the study of <a href="/wiki/Garbage_collection_(computer_science)" title="Garbage collection (computer science)">garbage collection</a>. The mutator abstracts the process that performs the computation, including allocation of a new storage cell. The collector is the process that automatically reclaims garbage. Furthermore, this paper gives a formalization of "<a href="/wiki/Tracing_garbage_collection#Tri-color_marking" title="Tracing garbage collection">tri-color marking</a>" that is basic to incremental garbage collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 1980s Dijkstra and <a href="/wiki/Carel_S._Scholten" title="Carel S. Scholten">Carel S. Scholten</a> proposed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%E2%80%93Scholten_algorithm" title="Dijkstra–Scholten algorithm">Dijkstra–Scholten algorithm</a> for detecting termination in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributed_systems" title="Distributed systems">distributed systems</a>.</p>
<p>In 1981 Dijkstra developed <a href="/wiki/Smoothsort" title="Smoothsort">smoothsort</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Comparison_sort" title="Comparison sort">comparison-based</a> <a href="/wiki/Sorting_algorithm" title="Sorting algorithm">sorting algorithm</a> and a variation of <a href="/wiki/Heapsort" title="Heapsort">heapsort</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compiler_construction_and_programming_language_research">Compiler construction and programming language research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Compiler construction and programming language research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg/180px-Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg/270px-Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg/360px-Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
By August 1960, Dijkstra and his colleague Zonneveld had completed the <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60#ALGOL_60_implementations_timeline" title="ALGOL 60">implementation of the first ALGOL 60 compiler</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Electrologica_X1" title="Electrologica X1">Electrologica X1</a> computer.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Dijkstra was known to be a fan of <a href="/wiki/ALGOL" title="ALGOL">ALGOL 60</a>, and worked on the team that implemented the first <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> for that language. He was closely involved in the ALGOL 60 development, realisation and popularisation. As discussed by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Naur" title="Peter Naur">Peter Naur</a> in the article 'The European side of the last phase of the development of ALGOL 60', in the <i>Proceedings of the First ACM SIGPLAN Conference on History of Programming Languages</i>, January 1978, Dijkstra took part in the period 1958–1959 in a number of meetings that culminated in the publication of the report defining the ALGOL 60 language. Dijkstra's name does not appear in the list of 13 authors of the final report. Apparently, he eventually left the committee because he could not agree with the majority opinions. Still, while at the Mathematisch Centrum (Amsterdam), he wrote jointly with <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jaap_Zonneveld&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jaap Zonneveld (page does not exist)">Jaap Zonneveld</a> the <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60#ALGOL_60_implementations_timeline" title="ALGOL 60">first ALGOL 60 compiler</a>. Dijkstra and Zonneveld, who collaborated on the compiler, agreed not to shave until the project was completed; while Zonneveld shaved shortly thereafter, Dijkstra kept his beard for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/ALGOL" title="ALGOL">ALGOL</a> was the result of a collaboration of American and European committees. <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a> (short for ALGOrithmic Language 1960) is a member of the ALGOL family of computer programming languages. It followed on from <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_58" title="ALGOL 58">ALGOL 58</a> and inspired many languages that followed it. It gave rise to many other programming languages, including <a href="/wiki/BCPL" title="BCPL">BCPL</a>, <a href="/wiki/B_(programming_language)" title="B (programming language)">B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simula" title="Simula">Simula</a> and <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Algol 60 was a sophisticatedly designed <a href="/wiki/Computer_language" title="Computer language">computer language</a> and it provided a large number of hitherto unknown <a href="/wiki/Programming_language_implementation" title="Programming language implementation">implementation</a> challenges. As <a href="/wiki/Bjarne_Stroustrup" title="Bjarne Stroustrup">Bjarne Stroustrup</a> notes, "one problem with Algol60 was that no one knew how to implement it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> A major new challenge in <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60#ALGOL_60_implementations_timeline" title="ALGOL 60">Algol 60 implementation</a> was the run-time allocation and management of data. In 1960 Dijkstra and Zonneveld showed how recursive procedures could be executed using a run-time stack of activation records, and how to efficiently access identifiers from statically enclosing scopes using the so-called 'display'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The ALGOL 60 compiler was one of the first to support <a href="/wiki/Recursion_(computer_science)" title="Recursion (computer science)">recursion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> employing a novel method to do so. Dijkstra's short book <i>Primer of Algol 60 Programming</i>, originally published in 1962, was the standard reference for the language for several years.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Programming_paradigm_and_methodology">Programming paradigm and methodology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Programming paradigm and methodology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">Computer programming</a> in the 1950s to 1960s was not recognized as an <a href="/wiki/Discipline_(academia)" title="Discipline (academia)">academic discipline</a> and unlike mature sciences there were no theoretical concepts or coding systems. Programming as a professional activity was poorly understood in those years. In Dijkstra's own words:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ewd340_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What about the poor <a href="/wiki/Programmer" title="Programmer">programmer</a>? Well, to tell the honest truth: he was hardly noticed. For one thing, the first machines were so bulky that you could hardly move them and besides that, they required such extensive maintenance that it was quite natural that the place where people tried to use the machine was the same laboratory where the machine had been developed. Secondly, his somewhat invisible work was without any glamour: you could show the machine to visitors and that was several orders of magnitude more spectacular than some sheets of coding. But most important of all, the programmer himself had a very modest view of his own work: his work derived all its significance from the existence of that wonderful machine. Because that was a unique machine, he knew only too well that his programs had only local significance and also, because it was patently obvious that this machine would have a limited lifetime, he knew that very little of his work would have a lasting value.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In the late 1960s computer programming was in a <a href="/wiki/Software_crisis" title="Software crisis">state of crisis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Software_crisis" title="Software crisis">Software crisis</a> is a term used in the early days of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computing_science" title="Computing science">computing science</a> for the difficulty of writing useful and efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_programs" title="Computer programs">computer programs</a> in the required time. The software crisis was due to the rapid increases in computer power and the complexity of the problems that could be tackled. With the increase in the complexity of the <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a>, many software problems arose because existing methods were neither sufficient nor up to the mark. The term <i>software crisis" was coined by some attendees at the first <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> Software Engineering Conference in 1968 at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garmisch" title="Garmisch">Garmisch</a>, Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haigh.2C_Thomas_2010_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Haigh.2C_Thomas_2010-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nato_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-nato-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nato2_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-nato2-61">[61]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edsger_Dijkstra" title="Edsger Dijkstra">Edsger Dijkstra</a>'s 1972 ACM Turing Award Lecture makes reference to this same problem: "The major cause of the software crisis is that the machines have become several orders of magnitude more powerful! To put it quite bluntly: as long as there were no machines, programming was no problem at all; when we had a few weak computers, programming became a mild problem, and now we have gigantic computers, programming has become an equally gigantic problem."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ewd340_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-32">[32]</a></sup></i></p>
<p>While Dijkstra had programmed extensively in machine code in the 1950s, he came to the conclusion that in high-level languages frequent use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GOTO" title="GOTO">GOTO</a> statement was usually symptomatic of poor structure. In 1968 he wrote a private paper "A Case against the GO TO Statement",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> which was then published as a letter in CACM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Editor <a href="/wiki/Niklaus_Wirth" title="Niklaus Wirth">Niklaus Wirth</a> gave this letter the heading "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_To_Statement_Considered_Harmful" title="Go To Statement Considered Harmful">Go To Statement Considered Harmful</a>", which introduced the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Considered_harmful" title="Considered harmful">considered harmful</a>" into computing.</p>
<p>Dijkstra argued that the programming statement <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GOTO" title="GOTO">GOTO</a>, found in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-level_programming_languages" title="High-level programming languages">high-level programming languages</a>, is a major source of errors, and should therefore be eliminated. This letter caused a huge debate in the programming community. Some went to the length of equating good programming with the elimination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GO_TO" title="GO TO">GO TO</a>. Dijkstra refused to mention the debate, or even the GO TO statement, in his seminal article, "Notes on Structured Programming". The debate has long since died down; programming languages provide alternatives to the GO TO, few programmers today use it liberally, and most never use it at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-9"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fortran" title="Fortran">Fortran</a> introduced structured programming constructs in 1978 and in successive revisions the relatively loose semantic rules governing the allowable use of goto were tightened; the "extended range" in which a programmer could use a GOTO to enter and leave a still-executing DO loop was removed from the language in 1978, and by 1995 several forms of Fortran GOTO, including the Computed GOTO and the Assigned GOTO, had been deleted from the language. Some widely used modern programming languages, such as <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a> and <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a> lack the GOTO statement, though most provide some means of breaking out of a selection, or either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Break_statement" title="Break statement">breaking</a> out of or <a href="/wiki/Control_flow#Continuation_with_next_iteration" title="Control flow">moving</a> on to the next step of an iteration.</p>
<p>Dijkstra's thesis was that departures from linear control flow were clearer if allowed only in disciplined higher-level structures such as the <a href="/wiki/Conditional_(computer_programming)" title="Conditional (computer programming)">if-then-else</a> statement and the <a href="/wiki/While_loop" title="While loop">while loop</a>. This methodology was developed into <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming movement</a>, the title of his 1972 book, coauthored with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C.A.R._Hoare" title="C.A.R. Hoare">C.A.R. Hoare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ole-Johan_Dahl" title="Ole-Johan Dahl">Ole-Johan Dahl</a>. Considered by many as the first significant movement in history of computer programming, structured programming became the new programming orthodoxy during the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Meyer" title="Bertrand Meyer">Bertrand Meyer</a> remarked: "The revolution in views of programming started by Dijkstra's iconoclasm led to a movement known as structured programming, which advocated a systematic, rational approach to program construction. Structured programming is the basis for all that has been done since in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" title="Programming methodology">programming methodology</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented programming</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">Structured programming</a> is often regarded as "goto-less programming". But as Bertrand Meyer notes: "As the first book on the topic [<i>Structured Programming</i> by Dijkstra, Dahl, and Hoare] shows, structured programming is about much more than control structures and the <a href="/wiki/Goto" title="Goto">goto</a>. Its principal message is that programming should be considered a scientific discipline based on mathematical rigor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">As a programming paradigm</a>, structured programming – especially in the 1970s and 1980s – significantly influenced the birth of many modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Programming_languages" title="Programming languages">programming languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a>, <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modula-2" title="Modula-2">Modula-2</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ada_(programming_language)" title="Ada (programming language)">Ada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fortran_77" title="Fortran 77">Fortran 77</a> version which incorporates the concepts of structured programming, was released in 1978. The <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> language was a considerably extended and enhanced version of the popular <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">structured programming language</a> <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a> (see also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_C-based_programming_languages" title="List of C-based programming languages">list of C-based programming languages</a>). Since C++ was developed from a more traditional <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">structured language</a>, it is a '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-paradigm_programming_language" title="Multi-paradigm programming language">hybrid language</a>', rather than a pure <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented programming</a> language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dijkstra was particularly impressed by the dimension of the software problem when he attended the famous 1968 NATO Conference on Software Engineering, which was the first conference devoted to the problem. He claimed that programming as a <a href="/wiki/Discipline_(academia)" title="Discipline (academia)">discipline</a> in contrast to a <a href="/wiki/Craft" title="Craft">craft</a>, that programming must no longer be an ad hoc activity, and that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" title="Programming methodology">programming methodology</a> should become a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_discipline" title="Scientific discipline">scientific discipline</a>. Dijkstra formulated some of his early ideas about programming as a mathematical discipline starting around the time of publication of his letter ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_To_Statement_Considered_Harmful" title="Go To Statement Considered Harmful">Go To Statement Considered Harmful</a>"). He continued to point out that <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Software_productivity&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Software productivity (page does not exist)">software productivity</a> is closely related to rigor in design, which eliminates software bugs at an early stage.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Program_design_and_development_.28software_engineering_research.29">Program design and development (software engineering research)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Program design and development (software engineering research)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dijkstra's ideas about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" title="Programming methodology">programming methodology</a> (especially the structured programming movement) helped lay the foundations for the birth and development of the professional discipline of <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> (in particular the software design and development), enabling programmers to organize and manage increasingly complex software projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In the late 1960s Dijkstra discussed the concept of program families. And in the mid 1970s <a href="/wiki/David_Parnas" title="David Parnas">David Parnas</a> and others clarified the idea and showed how to apply it in software engineering principles.</p>
<p>Dijkstra sent "Notes on Structured Programming", later to be one of his seminal works, to a few friends soliciting their comments. This note became a sensation, and major corporations initiated programs based on his ideas to integrate rigorous practices into their programming projects. Dijkstra had advocated certain design principles, which have now become completely accepted in the computer science community: Large systems should be constructed out of many smaller components; each component should be defined only by its interface and not by its implementation; smaller components may be designed following a similar process of decomposition, thus leading to a top-down style of design; the design should start by enumerating the "<a href="/wiki/Separation_of_concerns" title="Separation of concerns">separable concerns</a>" and by considering each group of concerns independently of the others; and mathematical logic is and must be the basis for software design. This work has had far-reaching impact on all areas of computer science, from teaching of the very first course in programming to the designs of complex software. Mathematical analysis of designs and specifications have become central activities in computer science research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-10"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rise of the <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming movement</a> led to many other <a href="/wiki/Structured_analysis" title="Structured analysis"><i>structured</i> approaches</a> applied to <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">software design</a>. The techniques of <a href="/wiki/Structured_analysis" title="Structured analysis">structured analysis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structured_design" title="Structured design">structured design</a> are outgrowths of <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming</a> concepts and techniques, and of the early ideas about modular design. Principles of modularity were strengthened by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Constantine" title="Larry Constantine">Larry Constantine</a>'s concepts of <a href="/wiki/Coupling_(computer_programming)" title="Coupling (computer programming)">coupling</a> (to be minimized between modules) and <a href="/wiki/Cohesion_(computer_science)" title="Cohesion (computer science)">cohesion</a> (to be maximized within modules), by <a href="/wiki/David_Parnas" title="David Parnas">David Parnas</a>'s techniques of <a href="/wiki/Information_hiding" title="Information hiding">information hiding</a>, and by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abstract_data_types" title="Abstract data types">abstract data types</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> A number of tools and methods employing <a href="/wiki/Structured_analysis" title="Structured analysis">structured concepts</a> were developed, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structured_Design" title="Structured Design">Structured Design</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jackson_Structured_Programming" title="Jackson Structured Programming">Jackson's Structured Programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_T._Ross" title="Douglas T. Ross">Ross</a>' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structured_Analysis_and_Design_Technique" title="Structured Analysis and Design Technique">Structured Analysis and Design Technique</a> (SADT), <a href="/wiki/Edward_Yourdon#Yourdon_Structured_Method" title="Edward Yourdon">Yourdon's Structured Method</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structured_Systems_Analysis_and_Design_Method" title="Structured Systems Analysis and Design Method">Structured Systems Analysis and Design Method</a> (SSADM), and James Martin's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Information_Engineering" title="Information Engineering">Information Engineering</a>. The field of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Software_metrics" title="Software metrics">software metrics</a> is often considered as a direct influence of the structured programming movement on <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> in the 1970s.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Separation_of_concerns" title="Separation of concerns">Separation of concerns</a> (SoC), one of the basic principles in <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a>, is a design principle for separating a <a href="/wiki/Computer_program" title="Computer program">computer program</a> into distinct sections, such that each section addresses a separate <a href="/wiki/Concern_(computer_science)" title="Concern (computer science)">concern</a>. The term <i><a href="/wiki/Separation_of_concerns" title="Separation of concerns">separation of concerns</a></i> was coined by Dijkstra in his 1974 paper "On the role of scientific thought".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Let me try to explain to you, what to my taste is characteristic for all intelligent thinking. It is, that one is willing to study in depth an aspect of one's subject matter in isolation for the sake of its own consistency, all the time knowing that one is occupying oneself only with one of the aspects. We know that a program must be correct and we can study it from that viewpoint only; we also know that it should be efficient and we can study its efficiency on another day, so to speak. In another mood we may ask ourselves whether, and if so: why, the program is desirable. But nothing is gained —on the contrary!— by tackling these various aspects simultaneously. It is what I sometimes have called <b>"the separation of concerns"</b>, which, even if not perfectly possible, is yet the only available technique for effective ordering of one's thoughts, that I know of. This is what I mean by "focusing one's attention upon some aspect": it does not mean ignoring the other aspects, it is just doing justice to the fact that from this aspect's point of view, the other is irrelevant. It is being one- and multiple-track minded simultaneously.</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operating_system_research">Operating system research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Operating system research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1960s Dijkstra and his colleagues in <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a> designed and implemented <a href="/wiki/THE_multiprogramming_system" title="THE multiprogramming system">THE</a> (standing for '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technische_Hogeschool_Eindhoven" title="Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven">Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven</a>') <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a>, which was organised into clearly identified <a href="/wiki/Abstraction_layer" title="Abstraction layer">layers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> His 1968 article on this subject provided the foundation for subsequent designs of the operating systems.</p>
<p>Dijkstra organized the design of the system in layers in order to reduce the overall complexity of the software. Though the term 'architecture' had not yet been used to describe <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">software design</a>, this was certainly considered the first glimpse of <a href="/wiki/Software_architecture" title="Software architecture">software architecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> It introduced a number of design principles which have become part of the working vocabulary of every professional programmer: levels of abstraction, programming in layers, the semaphore, and cooperating sequential processes. His original paper on the THE operating system was reprinted in the 25th Anniversary issue of Communications of the ACM, in January 1983. By way of introduction, the Editor-in-Chief says, "This project initiated a long line of research in multilevel systems architecture — a line that continues to the present day because hierarchical modularity is a powerful approach to organizing large systems."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-11"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concurrent_computing_and_programming">Concurrent computing and programming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Concurrent computing and programming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2048" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg/180px-Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg/270px-Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg/360px-Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <span lang="nl" xml:lang="nl"><i>seinpaal</i></span>, the term used in Dijkstra's original description<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>). In the early 1960s Dijkstra proposed the first synchronisation mechanism for concurrent processes, the <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> with its two operations, P and V.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In a one-page paper from 1965 Dijkstra introduced the '<a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> problem' for n processes and discussed a solution to it. It was probably the first published <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lamport_.282015.29_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lamport_.282015.29-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup> The notion, standard by now, of a '<a href="/wiki/Critical_section" title="Critical section">critical section</a>' was also introduced in this paper. <a href="/wiki/Per_Brinch_Hansen" title="Per Brinch Hansen">Per Brinch Hansen</a>, a pioneer in the field of <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">concurrent computing</a>, considers Dijkstra's <i>Cooperating Sequential Processes</i> (1965) to be the <a href="/wiki/List_of_important_publications_in_concurrent,_parallel,_and_distributed_computing" title="List of important publications in concurrent, parallel, and distributed computing">first classic paper</a> in concurrent programming. As Brinch Hansen notes, 'Dijkstra lays the conceptual foundation for abstract concurrent programming' with that paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1808" data-file-width="2550" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png/180px-Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png/270px-Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png/360px-Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A simple example of two processes modifying a linked list at the same time causing a conflict.
