The requirement of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> was first identified and solved by Dijkstra in his seminal 1965 paper titled <i>Solution of a problem in concurrent programming control</i>, and is credited as the first topic in the study of <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithms</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1968 Dijkstra published his seminal paper 'Cooperating sequential processes', a 70-page essay that originated the field of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concurrent_programming" title="Concurrent programming">concurrent programming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> He discussed in it the notion of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutex" title="Mutex">mutex</a>) and the criteria a satisfactory solution should satisfy. He also redressed the historical perspective left out of his 1965 paper by including the <a href="/wiki/Dekker%27s_algorithm" title="Dekker's algorithm">first known correct solution to the mutual exclusion problem</a>, for two processes, due to <a href="/wiki/Theodorus_Dekker" title="Theodorus Dekker">Theodorus Dekker</a>. Dijkstra subsequently generalized <a href="/wiki/Dekker%27s_algorithm" title="Dekker's algorithm">Dekker's solution</a> to n processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Further, he proposed the first synchronisation mechanism for concurrent processes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> with its two operations, P and V. He also identified the '<a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a> problem' (called there 'the problem of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deadly_embrace" title="Deadly embrace">deadly embrace</a>')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and proposed an elegant '<a href="/wiki/Banker%27s_algorithm" title="Banker's algorithm">Banker's algorithm</a>' that <a href="/wiki/Deadlock_prevention_algorithms" title="Deadlock prevention algorithms">prevents deadlock</a>. The deadlock detection and prevention became perennial research problems in the field of concurrent programming.</p>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/Programming_paradigm" title="Programming paradigm">programming paradigm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">concurrent computing</a> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Modular_programming" title="Modular programming">modular programming</a>, namely <a href="/wiki/Decomposition_(computer_science)" title="Decomposition (computer science)">factoring</a> an overall computation into subcomputations that may be executed concurrently.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1172" data-file-width="1130" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png/180px-An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png/270px-An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png/360px-An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration of the dining philosophers problem</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dining_philosophers_problem" title="Dining philosophers problem">dining philosophers problem</a> is an example problem often used in <a href="/wiki/Concurrency_(computer_science)" title="Concurrency (computer science)">concurrent</a> algorithm design to illustrate <a href="/wiki/Synchronization_(computer_science)" title="Synchronization (computer science)">synchronization</a> issues and techniques for resolving them. It was originally formulated in 1965 by Dijkstra as a student exam exercise, presented in terms of computers <a href="/wiki/Resource_contention" title="Resource contention">competing for access</a> to <a href="/wiki/Tape_drive" title="Tape drive">tape drive</a> peripherals. Soon after, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Tony Hoare</a> gave the problem its present formulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Several years after Dijkstra posed the problem, he was surprised to find that the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> designers of the sophisticated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MULTICS" title="MULTICS">MULTICS</a> operating system had not thought about <a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a> at all, and the system would, on occasion, abruptly stop – like so many philosophers each holding a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chopstick" title="Chopstick">chopstick</a>. With gentle irony, he recalled: "You can hardly blame M.I.T. for not taking notice of an obscure computer scientist in a small town (<a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a>) in the Netherlands."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sleeping_barber_problem" title="Sleeping barber problem">sleeping barber problem</a>, a classic <a href="/wiki/Inter-process_communication" title="Inter-process communication">inter-process communication</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synchronization" title="Synchronization">synchronization</a> problem between multiple <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a> <a href="/wiki/Process_(computing)" title="Process (computing)">processes</a>, is often attributed to Dijkstra.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distributed_computing">Distributed computing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Distributed computing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dijkstra was one of the very early pioneers of the research on principles of <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> As the citation for the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra_Prize" title="Dijkstra Prize">Dijkstra Prize</a> recognizes, "no other individual has had a larger influence on research in principles of distributed computing." Some of his papers are even considered to be those that established the field. Dijkstra's 1965 paper, <i>Solution of a Problem in Concurrent Programming Control</i> was the first to present the correct solution to the <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> problem. <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Lamport" title="Leslie Lamport">Leslie Lamport</a> writes that this work "is probably why <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PODC" title="PODC">PODC</a> exists" and it "started the field of concurrent and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithms" title="Distributed algorithms">distributed algorithms</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In particular, his paper "Self-stabilizing Systems in Spite of Distributed Control" (1974) started the sub-field of <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a>. It is also considered as the first scientific examination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fault-tolerant_systems" title="Fault-tolerant systems">fault-tolerant systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lamport_.282015.29_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lamport_.282015.29-12">[12]</a></sup> Dijkstra's paper was not widely noticed until Leslie Lamport's invited talk at the ACM <a href="/wiki/Symposium_on_Principles_of_Distributed_Computing" title="Symposium on Principles of Distributed Computing">Symposium on Principles of Distributed Computing</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PODC" title="PODC">PODC</a>) in 1983. In his report on Dijkstra's work on <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilizing</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributed_systems" title="Distributed systems">distributed systems</a>, Lamport regard it to be 'a milestone in work on <a href="/wiki/Fault_tolerance" title="Fault tolerance">fault tolerance</a>' and 'a very fertile field for research',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> as can be seen by studying the book <i>Self-stabilization</i> (2000) of <a href="/wiki/Shlomi_Dolev" title="Shlomi Dolev">Shlomi Dolev</a> and by browsing through the proceedings of the annual workshops on <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilizing systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Self-stabilization is related to <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_computing" title="Autonomic computing">autonomic computing</a>, which entails several "self-*" attributes: self-management, self-configuration, self-healing, self-optimization, and self-protection.</p>
<p>In the early 1980s he published two small papers on the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%E2%80%93Scholten_algorithm" title="Dijkstra–Scholten algorithm">problem of detecting termination in distributed systems</a>. The first one, four pages long, was written with Carel Scholten; the second, three pages long, with Wim Feijen and Netty van Gasteren.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_specification_and_verification">Formal specification and verification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Formal specification and verification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the 1970s, Dijkstra's chief interest was <a href="/wiki/Formal_verification" title="Formal verification">formal verification</a>. The prevailing opinion at the time was that one should first write a program and then provide a <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_proof" title="Mathematical proof">mathematical proof</a> of <a href="/wiki/Correctness_(computer_science)" title="Correctness (computer science)">correctness</a>. Dijkstra objected, noting that the resulting proofs are long and cumbersome and give no insight on how the program was developed. An alternative method is <i><a href="/wiki/Program_derivation" title="Program derivation">program derivation</a></i>, to "develop proof and program hand in hand". One starts with a mathematical <i>specification</i> of what a program is supposed to do and applies mathematical transformations to the specification until it is turned into a program that can be executed. The resulting program is then known to be <i>correct by construction</i>. Much of Dijkstra's later work concerns ways to streamline mathematical argument.</p>
<p>In 1976 Dijkstra published a seminal book, <i>A Discipline of Programming</i>, which put forward his method of systematic development of programs together with their correctness proofs. In his exposition he used his '<a href="/wiki/Guarded_Command_Language" title="Guarded Command Language">Guarded Command Language</a>'. The language, with its reliance on non-determinism, the adopted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weakest_precondition" title="Weakest precondition">weakest precondition</a> <a href="/wiki/Predicate_transformer_semantics" title="Predicate transformer semantics">semantics</a> and the proposed development method has had a considerable impact on the field to this day. The <a href="/wiki/Refinement_calculus" title="Refinement calculus">refinement calculus</a>, originally proposed by <a href="/wiki/Ralph-Johan_Back" title="Ralph-Johan Back">Ralph-Johan Back</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> and developed by <a href="/wiki/Carroll_Morgan_(computer_scientist)" title="Carroll Morgan (computer scientist)">Carroll Morgan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> is an extension of Dijkstra's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weakest_precondition_calculus" title="Weakest precondition calculus">weakest precondition calculus</a>, where program statements are modeled as <a href="/wiki/Predicate_transformer_semantics" title="Predicate transformer semantics">predicate transformers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1984, to add further support to this approach to programming, he published jointly with Wim Feijen an introductory textbook for first-year students of computer science. The book, first published in Dutch, was entitled <i>Een methode van programmeren</i>. The English edition appeared in 1988 as <i>A Method of Programming</i>.</p>
<p>Then in 1990 Dijkstra published his book <i>Predicate Calculus and Program Semantics</i> with <a href="/wiki/Carel_S._Scholten" title="Carel S. Scholten">Carel S. Scholten</a>. The book was devoted to logical and mathematical analysis of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weakest_precondition" title="Weakest precondition">weakest precondition</a> <a href="/wiki/Predicate_transformer_semantics" title="Predicate transformer semantics">semantics</a> with a long prelude concerning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weakest_precondition_calculus" title="Weakest precondition calculus">predicate calculus</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_nature_of_computer_science_and_computer_programming">On the nature of computer science and computer programming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: On the nature of computer science and computer programming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dijkstra wrote a large number of essays on the nature of computer science research and education, on its relationship to the industry and society, and even on the manner in which scientific articles should be written and presented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-12"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his 1988 paper <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Cruelty_of_Really_Teaching_Computer_Science" title="On the Cruelty of Really Teaching Computer Science">On the Cruelty of Really Teaching Computer Science</a></i>, Dijkstra argues that computer programming should be understood as a branch of mathematics, and that the formal provability of a program is a major criterion for correctness.</p>
<p>In a 2001 interview,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> he stated a desire for "elegance", whereby the correct approach would be to process thoughts mentally, rather than attempt to render them until they are complete. The analogy he made was to contrast the compositional approaches of <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Beethoven</a>.</p>
<p>Many of his opinions on computer science and programming have become widespread. For example, the programming phrase "two or more, use a for" (a rule of thumb when to use a loop) is sometimes attributed to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was the first to make the claim that programming is so inherently complex that, in order to manage it successfully, programmers need to harness every trick and abstraction possible.</p>
<p>Dijkstra was one of the most famous opponents of the engineering view of computing science. Like <a href="/wiki/Peter_Naur" title="Peter Naur">Peter Naur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kristen_Nygaard" title="Kristen Nygaard">Kristen Nygaard</a>, Dijkstra dislikes the very term 'computer science'. <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">Computer science</a>, as Dijkstra pointed out, deserves a better name. He suggests it can be called 'computing science'. Instead of the computer, or computing technology, Dijkstra wanted to emphasize the abstract mechanisms that computing science uses to master complexity. When expressing the abstract nature of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computing_science" title="Computing science">computing science</a>, he wrote,</p>
<dl>
<dd>A confusion of even longer standing came from the fact that the unprepared included the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_engineer" title="Electronic engineer">electronic engineers</a> that were supposed to design, build and maintain the machines. The job was actually beyond the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_technology" title="Electronic technology">electronic technology</a> of the day, and, as a result, the question of how to get and keep the physical equipment more or less in working condition became in the early days the all-overriding concern. As a result, the topic became – primarily in the USA – prematurely known as '<a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>' – which, actually, is like referring to <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a> as 'knife science' – and it was firmly implanted in people's minds that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computing_science" title="Computing science">computing science</a> is about machines and their peripheral equipment. Quod non [Latin: "Which is not true"]. We now know that electronic technology has no more to contribute to <a href="/wiki/Computing" title="Computing">computing</a> than the physical equipment. We now know that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Programmable_computer" title="Programmable computer">programmable computer</a> is no more and no less than an extremely handy device for realizing any conceivable mechanism without changing a single wire, and that the core challenge for computing science is hence a conceptual one, viz., what (abstract) mechanisms we can conceive without getting lost in the complexities of our own making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>In <i>The Humble Programmer</i> (1972), Dijkstra wrote: "We must not forget that it is not our [computing scientists'] business to make programs, it is our business to design classes of computations that will display a desired behaviour."</p>
<p>Dijkstra also opposed the inclusion of <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> under the umbrella of academic computer science. He wrote that, "As economics is known as 'The Miserable Science', software engineering should be known as 'The Doomed Discipline', doomed because it cannot even approach its goal since its goal is self-contradictory." And "software engineering has accepted as its charter 'How to program if you cannot.<span style="padding-right:.2em;">'</span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality_and_working_style">Personality and working style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Personality and working style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2447" data-file-width="3574" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg/220px-Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg/330px-Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg/440px-Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dijkstra at the blackboard during a conference at <a href="/wiki/ETH_Zurich" title="ETH Zurich">ETH Zurich</a> in 1994</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the world of computing science, Dijkstra is well-known as a "character". In the preface of his book <i>A Discipline of Programming</i> (1976) he stated the following: "For the absence of a bibliography I offer neither explanation nor apology." In fact, most of his articles and books have no references at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup> This approach to references was deplored by some researchers. But Dijkstra chose this way of working to preserve his self-reliance.</p>
<p>As a university professor for much of his life, Dijkstra saw teaching not just as a required activity but as a serious research endeavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-13"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup> His approach to teaching was unconventional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> His lecturing style has been described as idiosyncratic. When lecturing, the long pauses between sentences have often been attributed to the fact that English is not Dijkstra's first language. However the pauses also served as a way for him to think on his feet and he was regarded as a quick and deep thinker while engaged in the act of lecturing. His courses for students in Austin had little to do with computer science but they dealt with the presentation of mathematical proofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup> At the beginning of each <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semester" title="Semester">semester</a> he would take a photo of each of the students, in order to memorize their names. He never followed a <a href="/wiki/Textbook" title="Textbook">textbook</a>, with the possible exception of his own while it was under preparation. When lecturing, he would write proofs in chalk on a blackboard rather than using overhead foils. He invited the students to suggest ideas, which he then explored, or refused to explore because they violated some of his tenets. He assigned challenging homework problems, and would study his students' solutions thoroughly. He conducted his final examinations orally, over a whole week. Each student was examined in Dijkstra's office or home, and an exam lasted several hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-14"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was also highly original in his way of assessing people's capacity for a job. When Vladimir Lifschitz came to Austin in 1990 for a job interview, Dijkstra gave him a puzzle. Vladimir solved it and has been working in Austin since then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-8"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite having invented much of the technology of software, Dijkstra eschewed the use of computers in his own work for many decades. Even after he succumbed to his UT colleagues' encouragement and acquired a <a href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">Macintosh</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a>, he used it only for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-mail" title="E-mail">e-mail</a> and for browsing the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UTx_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-UTx-101">[101]</a></sup> Dijkstra never wrote his articles using a computer. He preferred to rely on his <a href="/wiki/Typewriter" title="Typewriter">typewriter</a> and later on his Montblanc pen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-9"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup> Dijkstra's favorite writing instrument was the Montblanc Meisterstück <a href="/wiki/Fountain_pen" title="Fountain pen">fountain pen</a>. He repeatedly tried other pens, but none ever displaced the Montblanc.</p>
<p>He had no use for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Word_processors" title="Word processors">word processors</a>, believing that one should be able to write a letter or article without rough drafts, rewriting, or any significant editing. He would work it all out in his head before putting <a href="/wiki/Pen" title="Pen">pen</a> to paper, and once mentioned that when he was a physics student he would solve his homework problems in his head while walking the streets of <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">Leiden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-15"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup> Most of Dijkstra's publications were written by him alone. He never had a secretary and took care of all his correspondence alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-10"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup> When colleagues prepared a Festschrift for his sixtieth birthday, published by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Springer-Verlag" title="Springer-Verlag">Springer-Verlag</a>, he took the trouble to thank each of the 61 contributors separately, in a hand-written letter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-11"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout Dijkstra's career, his work was characterized by elegance and economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-12"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup> A prolific writer (especially as an essayist), Dijkstra authored more than 1,300 papers, many written by hand in his precise script. They were essays and parables; fairy tales and warnings; comprehensive explanation and pedagogical pretext. Most were about mathematics and computer science; others were trip reports that are more revealing about their author than about the people and places visited. It was his habit to copy each paper and circulate it to a small group of colleagues who would copy and forward the papers to another limited group of scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> His love affair with <a href="/wiki/Simplicity" title="Simplicity">simplicity</a> came at an early age and under his mother's guidance. He once said he had asked his mother whether trigonometry was a difficult topic. She replied that he must learn all the formulas and that furthermore if he required more than five lines to prove something, he was on the wrong track.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denken_als_Discpline_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denken_als_Discpline-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dijkstra was famous for his wit, eloquence, and way with words, such as in his remark, "The question of whether Machines Can Think (…) is about as relevant as the question of whether Submarines Can Swim."; his advice to a promising researcher, who asked how to select a topic for research, "Do only what only you can do".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-16"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup> Dijkstra was also known for his vocal criticism. As an outspoken and critical visionary, he strongly opposed the teaching of <a href="/wiki/BASIC" title="BASIC">BASIC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWD498_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWD498-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>In many of his more humorous essays, Dijkstra described a fictional company of which he served as chairman. The company was called Mathematics, Inc., a company that he imagined having <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercialized" title="Commercialized">commercialized</a> the production of mathematical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theorems" title="Theorems">theorems</a> in the same way that software companies had commercialized the production of computer programs. He invented a number of activities and challenges of Mathematics Inc. and documented them in several papers in the EWD series. The imaginary company had produced a proof of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riemann_Hypothesis" title="Riemann Hypothesis">Riemann Hypothesis</a> but then had great difficulties collecting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royalties" title="Royalties">royalties</a> from mathematicians who had proved results assuming the Riemann Hypothesis. The proof itself was a <a href="/wiki/Trade_secret" title="Trade secret">trade secret</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_EWD.7C475_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_EWD.7C475-105">[105]</a></sup> Many of the company's proofs were rushed out the door and then much of the company's effort had to be spent on <a href="/wiki/Software_maintenance" title="Software maintenance">maintenance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> A more successful effort was the Standard Proof for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pythagoras%27_Theorem" title="Pythagoras' Theorem">Pythagoras' Theorem</a>, that replaced the more than 100 incompatible existing proofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Dijkstra described Mathematics Inc. as "the most exciting and most miserable business ever conceived".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cite_EWD.7C475_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_EWD.7C475-105">[105]</a></sup> EWD 443 (1974) describes his fictional company as having over 75 percent of the world's market share.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="EWDs">EWDs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: EWDs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Dijkstra was well known for his habit of carefully composing manuscripts with his <a href="/wiki/Fountain_pen" title="Fountain pen">fountain pen</a>. The manuscripts are called EWDs, since Dijkstra numbered them with <i>EWD</i>, his initials, as a prefix. According to Dijkstra himself, the EWDs started when he moved from the Mathematical Centre in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a> to the Eindhoven University of Technology (then Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven). After going to <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a>, Dijkstra experienced a <a href="/wiki/Writer%27s_block" title="Writer's block">writer's block</a> for more than a year. Dijkstra distributed photocopies of a new EWD among his colleagues. Many recipients photocopied and forwarded their copies, so the EWDs spread throughout the international computer science community. The topics were computer science and mathematics, and included trip reports, letters, and speeches. These short articles span a period of 40 years. Almost all EWDs appearing after 1972 were hand-written. They are rarely longer than 15 pages and are consecutively numbered. The last one, No. 1318, is from 14 April 2002. Within computer science they are known as the EWD reports, or, simply the EWDs. More than 1300 EWDs have been scanned, with a growing number transcribed to facilitate search, and are available online at the Dijkstra archive of the University of Texas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Dijkstra's self-confidence went together with a remarkably modest <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_(sociology)" title="Lifestyle (sociology)">lifestyle</a>, to the point of being <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/spartan" title="wikt:spartan">spartan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-13"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup> His and his wife's house in <a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a> was simple, small and unassuming. He did not own a TV, a VCR or a mobile telephone, and did not go to the movies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_17-14"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-17">[17]</a></sup> In contrast, he played the piano well and, while in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a>, liked to go to <a href="/wiki/Concert" title="Concert">concerts</a>. An enthusiastic listener of <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical music</a>, Dijkstra's favorite composer was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mozart" title="Mozart">Mozart</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-17"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quotes">Quotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Quotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-style" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Quotations#Overusing_quotations" title="Wikipedia:Quotations">too many or too-lengthy quotations</a> for an encyclopedic entry</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">help improve the article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Editing" title="Help:Editing">editing it</a> to take facts from excessively quoted material and rewrite them as <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">sourced</a> original prose. Consider transferring direct quotations to <a href="/wiki/Wikiquote" title="Wikiquote">Wikiquote</a>.</span> <small><i>(December 2015)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">
<p>The question of whether Machines Can Think (…) is about as relevant as the question of whether Submarines Can Swim.</p>
</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>— Dijkstra (1984), <i>The threats to computing science (EWD898)</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWD898_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWD898-111">[111]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<ul>
<li>"Automatic computers have now been with us for a quarter of a century. They have had a great impact on our society in their capacity of tools, but in that capacity their influence will be but a ripple on the surface of our culture, compared with the much more profound influence they will have in their capacity of intellectual challenge without precedent in the cultural history of mankind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ewd340_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Programming is one of the most difficult branches of applied mathematics; the poorer mathematicians had better remain pure mathematicians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWD498_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-EWD498-104">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li>"The tools we use have a profound (and devious!) influence on our thinking habits, and, therefore, on our thinking abilities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWD498_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-EWD498-104">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Brainpower is by far our scarcest resource."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ewd340_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>"The competent programmer is fully aware of the strictly limited size of his own skull; therefore he approaches the programming task in full humility, and among other things he avoids clever tricks like the plague."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ewd340_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>"I see no meaningful difference between programming methodology and mathematical methodology in general."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Program testing can be used to show the presence of bugs, but never to show their absence!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></li>
<li>"It is not the task of the University to offer what society asks for, but to give what society needs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></li>
<li>"We must give industry not what it wants, but what it needs." (Dijkstra quoted in the program of his birthday symposium, Austin, Texas, 2000)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-18"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Simplicity is prerequisite for reliability." (Handwritten annotation)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></li>
<li>"A picture may be worth a thousand words, a formula is worth a thousand pictures."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Do only what only you can do." (Dijkstra's advice to a promising researcher, who asked how to select a topic for research.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-19"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_and_recognition">Influence and recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Influence and recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">
<p>The difference between a computer programmer and a computer scientist is a job-title thing. Edsgar Dijkstra wants proudly to be called a "computer programmer," although he hasn't touched a computer now for some years. (...) His great strength is that he is uncompromising. It would make him physically ill to think of programming in C++.</p>
</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Donald_Knuth" title="Donald Knuth">Donald Knuth</a> (1996), an interview with Donald Knuth by Jack Woehr of <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Dobb%27s_Journal" title="Dr. Dobb's Journal">Dr. Dobb's Journal</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In 1972 the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">Association for Computing Machinery</a> (ACM) acknowledged Dijkstra's seminal contributions to the field by awarding him the distinguished Turing Award. The citation for the award reads:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DijkstraACMtribute_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraACMtribute-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Edsger Dijkstra was a principal contributor in the late 1950's to the development of the <a href="/wiki/ALGOL" title="ALGOL">ALGOL</a>, a high level programming language which has become a model of clarity and mathematical rigor. He is one of the principal exponents of the science and art of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Programming_languages" title="Programming languages">programming languages</a> in general, and has greatly contributed to our understanding of their structure, representation, and implementation. His fifteen years of publications extend from theoretical articles on <a href="/wiki/Graph_theory" title="Graph theory">graph theory</a> to basic manuals, expository texts, and philosophical contemplations in the field of programming languages.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The introduction given at the awards ceremony is a tribute to Dijkstra:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DijkstraACMtribute_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraACMtribute-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The working vocabulary of programmers everywhere is studded with words originated or forcefully promulgated by E.W. Dijkstra – display, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deadly_embrace" title="Deadly embrace">deadly embrace</a>, semaphore, go-to-less programming, <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming</a>. But his influence on programming is more pervasive than any glossary can possibly indicate. The precious gift that this Turing Award acknowledges is Dijkstra's style: his approach to programming as a high, intellectual challenge; his eloquent insistence and practical demonstration that programs should be composed correctly, not just debugged into <a href="/wiki/Correctness_(computer_science)" title="Correctness (computer science)">correctness</a>; and his illuminating perception of problems at the foundations of <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">program design</a>. (…) We have come to value good programs in much the same way as we value good literature. And at the center of this movement, creating and reflecting patterns no less beautiful than useful, stands E.W. Dijkstra.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In the words of Sir <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Tony Hoare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society#Fellow" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a>, delivered by him at Dijkstra's funeral:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-20"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Edsger is widely recognized as a man who has thought deeply about many deep questions; and among the deepest questions is that of traditional moral philosophy: How is it that a person should live their life? Edsger found his answer to this question early in his life: He decided he would live as an academic scientist, conducting research into a new branch of science, the science of computing. He would lay the foundations that would establish computing as a rigorous scientific discipline; and in his research and in his teaching and in his writing, he would pursue perfection to the exclusion of all other concerns. From these commitments he never deviated, and that is how he has made to his chosen subject of study the greatest contribution that any one person could make in any one lifetime.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In March 2003, the following email was sent to the <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> community:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>This is to announce that the award formerly known as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PODC_Influential-Paper_Award" title="PODC Influential-Paper Award">PODC Influential-Paper Award</a>" has been renamed the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edsger_W._Dijkstra_Prize_in_Distributed_Computing" title="Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing">Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing</a>" after the late Edsger W. Dijkstra, a pioneer in the area of distributed computing. His foundational work on concurrency primitives (such as the semaphore), concurrency problems (such as <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a>), reasoning about concurrent systems, and <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a> comprises one of the most important supports upon which the field of distributed computing is built. No other individual has had a larger influence on research in principles of distributed computing.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Former <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">ACM</a> President <a href="/wiki/Peter_J._Denning" title="Peter J. Denning">Peter J. Denning</a> wrote about Dijkstra:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Edsger Dijkstra, one of the giants of our field and a passionate believer in the mathematical view of programs and programming (...) Over the previous quarter-century, he had formulated many of the great intellectual challenges of the field as programming—the goto statement, structured programming, concurrent processes, semaphores, deadlocks, recursive programming in Algol, and deriving correct programs.</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Awards and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Among Dijkstra's awards and honors are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UTx_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-UTx-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Netherlands_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Royal Netherlands Academy of Arts and Sciences">Royal Netherlands Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> (1971)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></li>
<li>Distinguished Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/British_Computer_Society" title="British Computer Society">British Computer Society</a> (1971)</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">Association for Computing Machinery</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.M._Turing_Award" title="A.M. Turing Award">A.M. Turing Award</a> (1972)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harry_H._Goode_Memorial_Award" title="Harry H. Goode Memorial Award">Harry H. Goode Memorial Award</a> from the <a href="/wiki/IEEE_Computer_Society" title="IEEE Computer Society">IEEE Computer Society</a> (1974).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></li>
<li>Foreign Honorary Member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> (1975)</li>
<li>Doctor of Science <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honoris_Causa" title="Honoris Causa">Honoris Causa</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_University_Belfast" title="Queen's University Belfast">Queen's University Belfast</a> (1976)</li>
<li>Computer Pioneer Charter Recipient from the <a href="/wiki/IEEE_Computer_Society" title="IEEE Computer Society">IEEE Computer Society</a> (1982)</li>
<li>ACM/<a href="/wiki/SIGCSE" title="SIGCSE">SIGCSE</a> Award for Outstanding Contributions to Computer Science Education (1989)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ACM_Fellow" title="ACM Fellow">Fellow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">Association for Computing Machinery</a> (1994)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></li>
<li>Honorary doctorate from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athens_University_of_Economics_%26_Business" title="Athens University of Economics &amp; Business">Athens University of Economics &amp; Business</a>, Greece (2001).</li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/DFBCS" title="DFBCS">Distinguished Fellowship of the British Computer Society</a> (BCS) is awarded under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bylaw" title="Bylaw">bylaw</a> 7 of the <a href="/wiki/British_Computer_Society" title="British Computer Society">BCS</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Charter" title="Royal Charter">Royal Charter</a>. The award was first approved in 1969 and the first election was made in 1971 to Dijkstra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the occasion of Dijkstra's 60th birthday in 1990, The Department of Computer Science (UTCS) at the University of Texas at Austin organized a two-day seminar in his honor. Speakers came from all over the United States and Europe, and a group of computer scientists contributed research articles which were edited into a book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_15-21"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002, the C&amp;C Foundation of Japan recognized Dijkstra "for his pioneering contributions to the establishment of the scientific basis for computer software through creative research in basic software theory, algorithm theory, structured programming, and semaphores." Dijkstra was alive to receive notice of the award, but it was accepted by his family in an award ceremony after his death.</p>
<p>Shortly before his death in 2002, Dijkstra received the ACM <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PODC_Influential-Paper_Award" title="PODC Influential-Paper Award">PODC Influential-Paper Award</a> in <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> for his work on self-stabilization of program computation. This annual award was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra_Prize" title="Dijkstra Prize">Dijkstra Prize</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edsger_W._Dijkstra_Prize_in_Distributed_Computing" title="Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing">Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing</a>) the following year, in his honor.</p>
<p>The Dijkstra Award for Outstanding Academic Achievement in Computer Science (<a href="/wiki/Loyola_University_Chicago" title="Loyola University Chicago">Loyola University Chicago</a>, Department of Computer Science) is named for Edger W. Dijkstra. Beginning in 2005, this award recognizes the top academic performance by a graduating computer science major. Selection is based on GPA in all major courses and election by department faculty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Department of Computer Science (UTCS) at the University of Texas at Austin hosted the inaugural Edsger W. Dijkstra Memorial Lecture on October 12, 2010. <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Tony Hoare</a>, Emeritus Professor at Oxford and Principal Researcher at Microsoft Research, was the speaker for the event. This lecture series was made possible by a generous grant from Schlumberger to honor the memory of Dijkstra.</p>

<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Hoare, C. A. R.</a> (12 October 2010). <a class="external text" href="https://www.cs.utexas.edu/users/EWD/DijkstraMemorialLectures/Tony%20Hoare.html" rel="nofollow">"<i>The 2010 Edsger W. Dijkstra Memorial Lecture: What Can We Learn from Edsger W. Dijkstra?</i>"</a>. Department of Computer Science, The University of Texas at Austin<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">12 August</span> 2015</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra&amp;rft.au=Hoare%2C+C.+A.+R.&amp;rft.btitle=The+2010+Edsger+W.+Dijkstra+Memorial+Lecture%3A+What+Can+We+Learn+from+Edsger+W.+Dijkstra%3F&amp;rft.date=2010-10-12&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cs.utexas.edu%2Fusers%2FEWD%2FDijkstraMemorialLectures%2FTony%2520Hoare.html&amp;rft.pub=Department+of+Computer+Science%2C+The+University+of+Texas+at+Austin&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ryder, Barbara G.; Soffa, Mary Lou; Burnett, Margaret (2005). <i>Impact of Software Engineering Research on Modern Programming Languages</i>. ACM Transactions on Software Engineering and Methodology, Vol. 14, No. 4, October 2005, p. 431-477. "Of great influence to <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a> was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structured_Programming" title="Structured Programming">Structured Programming</a>, put forth by E. W. Dijkstra. This method of proceeding in a design would obliviously be greatly encouraged by the use of a <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">Structured Language</a>, a language with a set of constructs that could freely be combined and nested. The textual structure of a program should directly reflect its flow of control."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Niklaus_Wirth" title="Niklaus Wirth">Wirth, Niklaus</a> (2008). <i>A Brief History of Software Engineering</i>. IEEE Annals of the History of Computing, vol.30, no. 3, July–September 2008, pp. 32–39. "In 1965 Dijkstra wrote his famous <i>Notes on Structured Programming</i> and declared programming as a discipline in contrast to a craft. Also in 1965 Hoare published an important paper about data structuring. These ideas had a profound influence on new programming language, in particular <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a>. Languages are the vehicles in which these ideas were to be expressed. <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">Structured programming</a> became supported by a <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">structured programming language</a>."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his 2004 memoir, "A Programmer's Story: The Life of a Computer Pioneer", Brinch Hansen wrote that he used "Cooperating Sequential Processes" to guide his work implementing multiprogramming on the RC 4000, and described it saying, "One of the great works in computer programming, this masterpiece laid the conceptual foundation for concurrent programming."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As Lamport (2002) wrote, "Edsger W. Dijkstra started the field of concurrent and distributed algorithms with his 1965 CACM paper "Solution of a Problem in Concurrent Programming Control", in which he first stated and solved the mutual exclusion problem. That paper is probably why <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PODC" title="PODC">PODC</a> exists; it certainly inspired most of my work."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Lamport_.282015.29-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Lamport_.282015.29_12-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamport_.282015.29_12-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamport_.282015.29_12-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a href="/wiki/Leslie_Lamport" title="Leslie Lamport">Lamport, Leslie</a>. <a class="external text" href="http://cacm.acm.org/magazines/2015/6/187316-turing-lecture-the-computer-science-of-concurrency/fulltext" rel="nofollow">"<i>Turing Lecture: The Computer Science of Concurrency: The Early Years (Communications of the ACM, Vol. 58 No. 6, June 2015)</i>"</a>. <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">ACM</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">22 September</span> 2015</span>. <q>While concurrent program execution had been considered for years, the computer science of concurrency began with Edsger Dijkstra's seminal 1965 paper that introduced the mutual exclusion problem. (...) The first scientific examination of fault tolerance was Dijkstra's seminal 1974 paper on self-stabilization. (...) The ensuing decades have seen a huge growth of interest in concurrency—particularly in distributed systems. Looking back at the origins of the field, what stands out is the fundamental role played by Edsger Dijkstra, to whom this history is dedicated.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra&amp;rft.au=Lamport%2C+Leslie&amp;rft.btitle=Turing+Lecture%3A+The+Computer+Science+of+Concurrency%3A+The+Early+Years+%28Communications+of+the+ACM%2C+Vol.+58+No.+6%2C+June+2015%29&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fcacm.acm.org%2Fmagazines%2F2015%2F6%2F187316-turing-lecture-the-computer-science-of-concurrency%2Ffulltext&amp;rft.pub=ACM&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Lo Russo, Graziano (1997). <a class="external text" href="http://www.stlport.org/resources/StepanovUSA.html" rel="nofollow">"<i>An Interview with A. Stepanov (Edizioni Infomedia srl.)</i>"</a>. STLport<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">30 August</span> 2015</span>. <q><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Stepanov" title="Alexander Stepanov">Alexander Stepanov</a>: "...I also discovered books of two great computer scientists from whose work I learned the scientific foundation of my trade: <a href="/wiki/Donald_Knuth" title="Donald Knuth">Donald Knuth</a> and Edsger Dijkstra. Knuth taught me the answers. Dijkstra taught me the questions. Time and time again I come back to their works for new insights."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra&amp;rft.au=Lo+Russo%2C+Graziano+%281997%29&amp;rft.btitle=An+Interview+with+A.+Stepanov+%28Edizioni+Infomedia+srl.%29&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.stlport.org%2Fresources%2FStepanovUSA.html&amp;rft.pub=STLport&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/David_Gries" title="David Gries">Gries, David</a> (1978). <i>Programming Methodology: A Collection of Articles by Members of IFIP WG2.3</i> (New York: Springer Verlag), p. 7. "The working vocabulary of programmers everywhere is studded with words originated or forcefully promulgated by E. W. Dijkstra—display, deadly embrace,semaphore, go-to-less programming, structured programming. But his influence on programming is more pervasive than any glossary can possibly indicate."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Donald_Knuth" title="Donald Knuth">Knuth, Donald</a> (1974). <i>Structured Programming with Go To Statements</i>. Computing Surveys 6 (4): 261–301. doi:10.1145/356635.356640. "A revolution is taking place in the way we write programs and teach programming, because we are beginning to understand the associated mental processes more deeply. It is impossible to read the recent [E. W. Dijkstra, O.-J. Dahl, and C. A. R. Hoare] book <i>Structured Programming</i>, without having it change your life. The reason for this revolution and its future prospects have been aptly described by E.W. Dijkstra in his 1972 Turing Award Lecture, <i>The Humble Programmer</i>."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-ewd340-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ewd340_32-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ewd340_32-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ewd340_32-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ewd340_32-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ewd340_32-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ewd340_32-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.cs.utexas.edu/users/EWD/transcriptions/EWD03xx/EWD340.html" rel="nofollow">E. <li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.cs.utexas.edu/users/EWD/transcriptions/EWD03xx/EWD340.html" rel="nofollow">E. W. Dijkstra Archive</a>. "Another two years later, in 1957, I married and Dutch marriage rites require you to state your profession and I stated that I was a programmer. But the municipal authorities of the town of Amsterdam did not accept it on the grounds that there was no such profession. And, believe it or not, but under the heading "profession" my marriage act shows the ridiculous entry "theoretical physicist"!"</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.podc.org/influential/2002-influential-paper/" rel="nofollow">2002 PODC Influential Paper Award (ACM Symposium on Principles of Distributed Computing)</a>. The following is a description of the winning paper's contribution, written by <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Lamport" title="Leslie Lamport">Leslie Lamport</a>: "Edsger W. Dijkstra started the field of concurrent and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithms" title="Distributed algorithms">distributed algorithms</a> with his 1965 CACM paper "Solution of a Problem in Concurrent Programming Control", in which he first stated and solved the mutual exclusion problem. That paper is probably why PODC exists; it certainly inspired most of my work. It remains to this day the most influential paper in the field."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-nilsson-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-nilsson_44-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Hart, P. E.; Nilsson, N. J.; Raphael, B. (1968). "A Formal Basis for the Heuristic Determination of Minimum Cost Paths". <i><a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Electrical_and_Electronics_Engineers" title="Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers">IEEE</a> Transactions on Systems Science and Cybernetics SSC4</i>. <b>4</b> (2): 100–107. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1109%2FTSSC.1968.300136" rel="nofollow">10.1109/TSSC.1968.300136</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra&amp;rft.atitle=A+Formal+Basis+for+the+Heuristic+Determination+of+Minimum+Cost+Paths&amp;rft.aufirst=P.+E.&amp;rft.aulast=Hart&amp;rft.au=Nilsson%2C+N.+J.&amp;rft.au=Raphael%2C+B.&amp;rft.date=1968&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1109%2FTSSC.1968.300136&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jtitle=IEEE+Transactions+on+Systems+Science+and+Cybernetics+SSC4&amp;rft.pages=100-107&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=4"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Frana, Philip L. (2001). "An Interview with Edsger W. Dijkstra (OH 330)". Communications of the ACM 53 (8): 41–47. doi:10.1145/1787234.1787249. "What is the shortest way to travel from Rotterdam to Groningen, in general: from given city to given city. It is the algorithm for the shortest path, which I designed in about twenty minutes. One morning I was shopping in Amsterdam with my young fiancée, and tired, we sat down on the café terrace to drink a cup of coffee and I was just thinking about whether I could do this, and I then designed the algorithm for the shortest path. As I said, it was a twenty-minute invention. In fact, it was published in '59, three years late. The publication is still readable, it is, in fact, quite nice. One of the reasons that it is so nice was that I designed it without pencil and paper. I learned later that one of the advantages of designing without pencil and paper is that you are almost forced to avoid all avoidable complexities. Eventually that algorithm became, to my great amazement, one of the cornerstones of my fame."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFDijkstra1959">Dijkstra, E. W. <li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Hudson, Richard (31 August 2015). <a class="external text" href="https://blog.golang.org/go15gc" rel="nofollow">"<i>Go GC: Prioritizing low latency and simplicity</i>"</a>. The Go Programming Language Blog<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">21 September</span> 2015</span>. <q><a href="/wiki/Go_(programming_language)" title="Go (programming language)">Go</a> is building a <a href="/wiki/Garbage_collection_(computer_science)" title="Garbage collection (computer science)">garbage collection</a> (GC) not only for 2015 but for 2025 and beyond: A GC that supports today's software development and scales along with new software and hardware throughout the next decade.<br/>
To create a garbage collector for the next decade, we turned to an algorithm from decades ago. Go's new garbage collector is a concurrent, tri-color, mark-sweep collector, an idea first proposed by Dijkstra in 1978. This is a deliberate divergence from most "enterprise" grade garbage collectors of today, and one that we believe is well suited to the properties of modern hardware and the latency requirements of modern software.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra&amp;rft.au=Hudson%2C+Richard&amp;rft.btitle=Go+GC%3A+Prioritizing+low+latency+and+simplicity&amp;rft.date=2015-08-31&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fblog.golang.org%2Fgo15gc&amp;rft.pub=The+Go+Programming+Language+Blog&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">22 December</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra&amp;rft.au=van+Emden%2C+Maarten&amp;rft.btitle=I+remember+Edsger+Dijkstra+%281930%932002%29&amp;rft.date=2008-05-06&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fvanemden.wordpress.com%2F2008%2F05%2F06%2Fi-remember-edsger-dijkstra-1930-2002%2F&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Daylight, E. G. <li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Dijkstra, Edsger W. <i>A Case against the GO TO Statement (EWD-215)</i>. <li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Dijkstra, E. W. (March 1968). "Letters to the editor: go to statement considered harmful". <i><a href="/wiki/Communications_of_the_ACM" title="Communications of the ACM">Communications of the ACM</a></i>. <b>11</b> (3): 147–148. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1145%2F362929.362947" rel="nofollow">10.1145/362929.362947</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0001-0782" rel="nofollow">0001-0782</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra&amp;rft.atitle=Letters+to+the+editor%3A+go+to+statement+considered+harmful&amp;rft.aufirst=E.+W.&amp;rft.aulast=Dijkstra&amp;rft.date=1968-03&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1145%2F362929.362947&amp;rft.issn=0001-0782&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Communications+of+the+ACM&amp;rft.pages=147-148&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=11"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Donald_Knuth" title="Donald Knuth">Knuth, Donald</a> (1974)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Harlan_Mills" title="Harlan Mills">Mills, Harlan D.</a> (1986). <i>Structured Programming: Retrospect and Prospect</i>. (IEEE Software 3(6): 58-66, November 1986). "Edsger W. Dijkstra's 1969 "Structured Programming" article precipitated a decade of intense focus on programming techniques that has fundamentally altered human expectations and achievements in software development.<br/>
Before this decade of intense focus, programming was regarded as a private, puzzle-solving activity of writing computer instructions to work as a program. After this decade, programming could be regarded as a public, mathematics-based activity of restructuring specifications into programs.<br/>
Before, the challenge was in getting programs to run at all, and then in getting them further debugged to do the right things. After, programs could be expected to both run and do the right things with little or no debugging. Before, it was common wisdom that no sizable program could be error-free. After, many sizable programs have run a year or more with no errors detected.<br/>
These expectations and achievements are not universal because of the inertia of industrial practices. But they are well-enough established to herald fundamental change in software development."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Reilly, Edwin D. (2004). <i>Concise Encyclopedia of Computer Science</i>. (John Wiley &amp; Sons, Ltd.), p. 734. "The major contributions of structured programming have been twofold—the elevation of programming technique to something less of an art and more of a science, and the demonstration that carefully structured programs can be creative works of sufficient literary merit to deserve being read by humans and not just by computer."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Nakagawa, Toru (TRIZ Journal, 2005)</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Broy, Manfred; Denert, Ernst (2002)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Henderson, Harry (Facts on File, Inc., 2009)</span></li>
